Dear Mr. Kalocsay:

Crossman Engineering, in accordance with our scope of services, has completed a traffic impact study for a proposed residential redevelopment project in the Town of Barrington, Rhode Island. The site is located to the immediate north of County Road (Route 103) on a large property formerly utilized for institutional purposes by the Barrington Colleg<sup>e</sup> and most recently by the Zion Bibl<sup>e</sup> Institute. The 39 plus acre parcel is partially developed with multiple buildings, including the historic Belton Court structure. Many of the college buildings that were in disrepair have recently been razed leaving the parcel primarily wooded with areas of disturbed open space.

Based upon our discussions, it is our understanding that an existing partially developed lot will be redeveloped to include a mixture of residential buildings including single family homes, townhouses and larger multi-unit buildings. A small neighborhood community space and retail component is also proposed to provide services for the residential village environment. Access to the property will be provided from three site accessroads intersecting with Middle Highway. The primary entrance to the new neighborhood will be located at the existing driveway intersection opposite Primrose Hill Road that will be modified to accommodate site changes in this immediate area.

The study included herein, was conducted to determine the adequacy of the existing servicing roadways to accommodate anticipated traffic to be generated by the residential redevelopment project. An analysis of potential impacts to the roadway capacity and safety has been completed and is discussed in the following report.

Very truly yours, Crossman Engineering

Paul J. Bannon Senior Project Director

# 1.0 INTRODUCTION

The objective of the following study is to assess the potential traffic impacts associated with a proposed redevelopment project in the Town of Barrington, Rhode Island. The existing 39.1-acre site, which is a remnant of the former Peck Estate, consists of six lots containing approximately 36.9 acres of land in Barrington and 2.2 acres in the City of East Providence. Refer to Figure 1 on the following page for project location in the community. The Peck Estat<sup>e</sup> purchased from Massasoit by Joseph Peck in the 1600's, encompassed over 800 acres between the Taunton River and Narragansett Bay. The portion of land within the Town of Barrington, was known as Ousamequi<sup>n</sup> Farm and was actively used for farming purposes during the early years. The property remained as ancestral land owned by the prominent Peck family that contributed substantially to the town during this period, until the 1940's when Frederick Peck donated the current 39-acre site and associated buildings to the Providence Homeopathic Hospital for medical use. Other portions of the expansive property along Primrose Hill Road and Middle Highway, were subdivided off for residential housing development which now surrounds the property, and 42 acres along Hundred Acre Cove were preserved by the Town of Barrington to create the Ousamequi<sup>n</sup> Natur<sup>e</sup> Trail<sup>s</sup> and Bird San<sup>c</sup>tuary.

There are multiple buildings on the site that were constructed during different periods of the early 1900's including the historic stone mansion buildings known as Belton Court built in 1906 and expanded in 1927. Other newer buildings were later constructed to repurpose the property for institutional use that included the Barrington Colleg<sup>e</sup> and Zion Bibl<sup>e</sup> Institute. Many of the newer class and dormitory buildings that were built for the educational facilities have been removed over the last three years due to their poor condition and incompatibility with the proposed residential reuse of the site.

It is the intent of the project to renovate a portion of the stone mansion for reuse as primarily a neighborhood public space for the village residents, and to construct a mixture of residential unit types from single family cottages to multiunit condominium buildings in a village environment. Primary access to the new neighborhood, Belton Court Villag<sup>e</sup>, will be provided on Middle Highway at the existing driveway entrance at the Primrose Hill Road intersection. Secondary access roads intersecting with Middle Highway are proposed at two locations along the property frontage.

The study summarized herein focused on both traffic flow efficiency and safety along Middle Highway, County Road and Primrose Hill Road in the immediate vicinity of the subject property, including their intersections. The potential impacts associated with the site related traffic have been defined and evaluated in accordance with standard traffic engineering guidelines and procedures.

The traffic engineering study completed for this project included the following:

• A traffic counting program to define the existing traffic patterns and operational characteristics along the servicing roadways. The data collection included an Automatic Traffic Recorder (ATR) counts on Middle Highway and Primrose Hill Road in the vicinity of the subject property and peak hour Turning Movement Counts(TMC) at the Middle Highway intersections with County Road and

![](_page_5_Figure_0.jpeg)

Primrose Hill Road. Record data from the Rhode Island Department of Transportation (RIDOT) was also reviewed.

- Inventory of the physical roadway characteristics of Middle Highway, County Road and Primrose Hill Road in the project area to determine the adequacy of the existing roadway geometric features in reference to safety and operations.
- Analysis of crash records obtained from the Barrington Police Department to determine if there are any safety concerns relative to the frequency, severity, or pattern of crashes in the project area.
- An estimate of future traffic volumes for the proposed residential redevelopment was determined through the use of data from the Trip Generation Manual, an informational report published by the Institute of Transportation Engineers (ITE).
- Evaluation and analysis of the traffic safety and operationsfor the future vehicular and pedestrian traffic conditions and development of recommendations where necessary, that would be required to maintain safe and efficient conditions in the project area.

# 2.0 PROJECT AREA

As noted in the previous section, the proposed redevelopment project situated on the former Zion Bibl<sup>e</sup> Institut<sup>e</sup> property, will include construction of a mixture of residential housing units in a village style environment. The site will contain a total of 344 residential units, a percentage of which will be reserved for senior housing. Additionally, the west wing of the stone manner containing approximately 20,000 square feet of available building area, will be repurposed predominately for common space accommodating neighborhood activities and management services for the village, with a small 1,500 square foot commercial element that would be focused on a small neighborhood business use. Figure 2 on the following page depicts the general project area, and the boundary lines of the subject property.

Land use in the immediate area can be defined as a predominately residential properties immediately surrounding the subject property and along Middle Highway and Primrose Hill Road. Further to the south there are recreational and institutional uses such as the Red Brick preschool, Primrose Hill Elementary School and St. James Church. The Sherwood baseball field complex issituated off of County Road adjacent to the church.

Middle Highway, County Road and Primrose Hill Road will serve as the primary access routes to the site. Based upon the good operating characteristics along these servicing roadways, and the estimated hourly volume and type of traffic associated with the redevelopment project, a study impact area was defined. The limits of the study area for analysis focused on these roadways in the immediate site vicinity including their intersections.

# 3.0 EXISTING CONDITIONS

# 3.1 ROADWAYS

## Middl<sup>e</sup> Highway

Middle Highway, a state-maintained roadway, runs north-south from Hickory Lane/Ormand Drive to Nayatt Road, a distance of 2.5 miles with a varying cross section and geometric features along its length. North of County Road (RI 103), Middle Highway classified as an urban major collector and south of County Road (RI 103) it is classified as an urban minor arterial. The roadway, posted at 25-mph, operates as a major north-south link through the community, paralleling both Route 114 to the east and Washington Road to the west.

In the project area, Middle Highway to the south of Primrose Hill Road is a two-lane road approximately 26 feet wide consisting of two 12-foot travel lanes and 1-foot shoulders delineated with a double yellow centerline and white shoulder markings. To the north of Primrose Hill Road, Middle Highway is variable in width 24 to 30 feet, and is delineated with narrower travel lanes. For much of its length this section of roadway is 30 feet wide consisting of 10-foot travel lanesin each direction. The shoulder width is generally 2 feet on the westerly side of the road with a greater 8-foot width on the easterly side.

The wide shoulder was constructed several years ago when this section of roadway was resurfaced. An existing bituminous sidewalk on the easterly side of the road was reconfigured to allow construction of the wider shoulder/sidewalk area with a berm instead of bituminous curbing, accommodating both pedestrians and bicyclist. To the south of Primrose Hill Road, an offset bituminous sidewalk in poor condition is provided along the easterly side of the road extending to Edgewood Drive. There is a short section of sidewalk missing between Edgewood Drive and the signalized intersection with County Road (RI 103) that provides concrete sidewalks on each corner of the intersection linking signal-controlled crosswalks. There are no sidewalks along the westerly side of the corridor north of County Road.

The town/state should investigate the feasibility of installing the missing shortsection(s) of sidewalk along the easterly side of the road in order to provide a continuous pedestrian route along Middle Highway in this area. Further south the Rhode Island Department of Transportation recently installed concrete sidewalks along the westerly side of Middle Highway from Sherwood Lane south, past Primros<sup>e</sup> Elem<sup>e</sup>ntary School and the Barrington Middl<sup>e</sup> School to the area of Maple Avenue, linking to the East Bay Bike Path at its southern terminus. The sidewalk was installed as part of a Safe Routes to School effort to enhance pedestrian safety and access in the vicinity of, and along routes to local schools. In the project area, there is one short missing section between County Road south to Sherwood Lane, similar to what is missing on the north side of the County Road intersection.

The pavement surface can be classified as being in good condition to the north as it was recently resurfaced, but to the south of Primrose Hill Road it can be classified as being in fair condition with a few limited areas of pavement in poor condition with distress caused by poor drainage. There are small sections of bituminous berm and concrete curb situated sporadically along both sides of the road to

control stormwater runoff, though adjacent erosion along the edge of pavement resulting from stormwater runoff was evident. There are utility poles with street lighting located on the westerly side of the corridor from the County Road (RI 103) area to the Primrose Hill Road intersection. North of the

intersection, the utility corridor with street lighting shifts to the westerly side of the roadway. These features of Middle Road in the vicinity of Edgewood Drive can be seen in the adjacent photograph.

## County Road (Rout<sup>e</sup> 103)

There are two distinct sections of County Road (RI 103) extending through the Town of Barrington. The primary route runs north-south from RI 114 at Wampanoag Trail

![](_page_9_Picture_6.jpeg)

through the center of town to Sowams Road at the Warren Bridge, a distance of 2.7 miles. The second section of County Road, in the general project area, runs in an east-west orientation extending from State Route 114 to Washington Road and Willett Avenue, a distance of 0.7 miles. The east/west roadway within the project area is posted at 30 mph and is classified as an urban principal arterial.

In the vicinity of the Middle Highway signalized intersection, County Road (RI 103) on the east extending to Route 114 is 26 feet wide consisting of 12-foot travel lanes and one-foot shoulders in each direction. To the west of the signalized intersection the two-lane roadway widens to 38 feet and provides for 13 foot travel lanes and 6-foot shoulders delineated with a double yellow centerline and white shoulder markings. There are no sidewalks along the corridor in the project area except for the aforementioned short area of sidewalk with curbing at each corner of the Middle Highway/County Road (RI 103) intersection. The pavementsurface can be classified as being in good to fair condition. Within the project limits, cobra-head light fixtures on utility poles are located on the north side of the street to the east of Middle Highway and on the south side of the street to the west of Middle Highway.

### Primrose Hill Road

Primrose Hill Road is short urban major collector road running east-west between Wampanoag Trail (RI 114) and Middle Highway, a distance of 0.4 miles. Primrose Hill Road, posted at 25 mph is stop-controlled on the westbound approach to Middle Highway and on the eastbound approach to Wampanoag Trail. In the project area, the road measures 26 feet in width consisting of an 11-foot travel lane and 2-foot shoulder in each direction delineated by a double yellow centerline and white shoulder markings. Bituminous berm extends along both sides of the roadway and no sidewalks are provided.

The pavement surface can be classified as being in good to fair condition with differing pavement ages as can be seen in the adjacent photograph where portions of the road had been resurfaced. Maintenance of the pavement surface has been completed as cracking sealing was performed to extend the pavement structure life. Within the project limits, cobrahead light fixtures on utility poles are located along the southerly side

![](_page_10_Picture_3.jpeg)

of the road for nighttime illumination of the roadway and side street intersections.

# 3.2 INTERSECTIONS

## Middl<sup>e</sup> Highway at County Road (RI 103)

Middle Highway intersects County Road to form a signalized, four-way junction. All approaches to the intersection provide a single all-purpose lane. The traffic signal system was upgraded several years ago along with pavement, curbing and sidewalk/curb ramps, and pavement markings. High visibility marked crosswalks were installed across all legs of the intersection including curb ramps and pedestrian signals to enhance pedestrian safety at the intersection.

The traffic signal equipment is in good condition having been installed in the last decade, as previously noted. The layout of the signal equipment consists of single mast arm mounted signal heads for each approach with in-road loop detectors. In addition, separate pedestal pole mounted pedestrian signal heads with pushbuttons including ADA compliant curb ramps, are provided on all legs of the intersection. The traffic signal was determined to operate in a fully actuated, two-phase manner with each roadway being serviced under their own phase and concurrent pedestrian movements.

### Middl<sup>e</sup> Highway at Primros<sup>e</sup> Hill Road

The major intersection under review as part of this study is the junction of the Middle Highway with Primrose Hill Road due its location in relation to the site and the proposed access driveways to the development. This intersection will service a large percentage of the site related traffic due to its location in the central portion of the site, and direct link to higher order roadways to the east. Primrose Hill Road currently intersects Middle Highway, to essentially form an unsignalized 3-way, "T" type junction with All-Way stop control. A fourth leg of the intersection that provides access to the subject property, historically and physically exists, but is not currently operational as it has been gated for a number of years to restrict access to the site. All approaches to the intersection provide a single all-purpose lane. There are stop signs/lines on each approach and no marked crosswalks are provided as there are no sidewalks within the intersection proper.

# 3.3 TRAFFIC DATA

Existing traffic flow characteristics for this area were developed from a traffic counting program conducted by Crossman and review of record data available from the RIDOT in the immediate project area. The data collection effort included 5-day Automatic Traffic Recorder (ATR) counts on Middle Highway and Primrose Hill Road, and peak hour (7:00-9:00 AM and 3:00-6:00 PM) Manual Turning Movement Counts (TMC) at the Middle Highway intersections with Primrose Hill Road and County Road during the weekday morning and afternoon peak periods in December 2023 and September 2024.

Based upon the ATR count program, Middle Highway north of County Road was found to service approximately 1,625 vehicles per day (vpd) on an average weekday and 1,450 vpd on a Saturday. On a typical weekday, traffic volumes begin to increase at 6:00 AM, with a morning peak hour of approximately 155 vehicles occurring between 8:00 and 9:00 AM. After 9:00 AM, the volumes decreased to between 75 and 130 vehicles per hour (vph), before increasing to the afternoon peak after 2:00 PM. The afternoon peak hour varied by day but generally occurred between the hours of 3:00 and 5:00 PM with similar peak volumes of approximately 155 vph, which is coincident with the school dismissal periods of the elementary and middle schools located along Middle Highway south of County Road.

Primrose Hill Road was found to service an average of approximately 1,025 vehicles per day during the week and approximately 910 vpd on a Saturday. On a typical weekday along the Primrose Hill Road, traffic volumes begin to increase at 6:00 AM, with a morning peak hour of approximately 80 vehicles occurring between 8:00 and 9:00 AM. After 9:00 AM, the volumes slightly decreased to between 60 and 80 vehicles per hour, remaining relatively constant until after 6:00 PM when they decline for the remainder of the day. Daily afternoon peak hours varied occurring during the mid-day and early afternoon period, but generally 80 vph are serviced during the peak hour.

In addition to the ATR counts, Crossman completed manual turning movement counts at the study intersections noted along Middle Highway. Data was collected during the peak weekday periods from 7:00 to 9:00 AM and 3:00 to 6:00 PM when the proposed site and surrounding roadways would service the highest combined peak volume of traffic. Figure 3 on the following page depicts the daily peak hour turning movement volumes for the morning and afternoon peak periods at the study intersections. The manual traffic count was found to be consistent with the ATR data for both roadways.

A review of RIDOT Seasonal Adjustment factors was also completed to determine average traffic conditions along the study roadways. It was determined that the data obtained on a weekday during the most recent count periods represent slightly higher than average traffic conditions along both roadways. Based on these factors, count data was not adjusted lower to represent average traffic conditions for our study, therefore a conservative analysis of traffic operations was completed. Complete count information can be found in the Appendix.

# 4.0 SAFETY ANALYSIS

In order to determine if there are any limiting factors affecting safety relating to access to the proposed residential project, the physical characteristics of Middle Highway in the project area were investigated. These limiting factors would potentially include horizontal or vertical roadway geometric changes or roadside obstructions that limit sight distances for vehicles traveling along the road or entering the road from a side street or driveway location. In this instance, the Stopping Sight Distanc<sup>e</sup> requirement is a design standard necessary to permit turning vehicles to safely enter and exit the development at the existing intersection with Primrose Hill Road, and at the proposed site access road intersections with Middle Highway. In addition to the review of physical roadway features, a review of record crash data from the Barrington Police Department was also completed to determine if there was a frequency, severity, or pattern of crashes in the project area that requires mitigation.

# STOPPING SIGHT DISTANCE

Stopping Sight Distance (SSD) is the minimum distance that a driver travelling along a roadway at or near the design speed, requires in order to adequately perceive, react and safely come to a stop prior to reaching an object in its travel path and avoid a collision. The available and required SSD are a function of the roadway geometry and design speed respectively, and are factored in when determining the appropriate and safe location of a site driveway or roadway intersection.

A review of the horizontal and vertical curvature of Middle Highway along the property frontage determined that the roadway can be described as generally straight and level within the project area

extending north of County Road through the Primrose Hill Road controlled junction. These features can be seen in the adjacent photograph looking north along the property frontage seen on the left.

Based upon the roadway geometry as described and the available sight distances determined through field analysis at the study intersections, a review of the required stopping sight distance was undertaken to ensure safe operations. The

![](_page_13_Picture_9.jpeg)

<sup>r</sup>equired SSD is based upon the speed of traffic travelling along the roadway and this value is compared to the available or <sup>m</sup>easured SSD to determine if this safety measure is satisfied. In determining the required SSD, the design speed of the roadway must be established. The most recent edition of the American Association of State Highway and Transportation Official's (AASHTO's) publication A Policy on

Geometri<sup>c</sup> Design <sup>o</sup>f Highway<sup>s</sup> and Streets, Tabl<sup>e</sup> 3-1 is referenced in determining the required stopping sight distances, which is based on the design speeds for the roadway.

One method of determining the design speed of a roadway is referenced in the RIDOT Highway Design Manual. On roadways with a posted speed limit less than 40 mph, the design speed is estimated to be the posted speed limit, plus 5 mph in urban areas, and plus 10 mph in rural areas. To determine if the minimum requirements for safe SSDs were met in this study, a design speed of 30 mph was utilized for evaluating the stopping sight distance on Middle Highway.

In addition to evaluating the SSD based upon the posted <sup>s</sup>peed limit and resultant design <sup>s</sup>peed, to be conservative at the Middle Highway site access road locations, actual speed data was obtained to determine the 85th percentile speed for drivers travelling along Middle Highway to the south of Primrose Hill Road. The 85th percentile speed represents the speed at which 85 percent of drivers are travelling at or slower, and is utilized when available in the analysis of required sight distances. Based on speed data obtained as part of the data collection program, the 85th percentile speed of traffic travelling along Middle Highway with a posted speed limit of 25 mph, was determined to be 33 miles per hour for northbound and southbound vehicles. It should be noted that travel speeds along the property frontage are highly variable and relatively low due to the All-Way stop-controlled intersection at Primrose Hill Road and the traffic signal control at County Road and range between 25 and 35 mph.

Based upon the roadway geometry as defined for Middle Highway, the sight distances available at the existing intersection of Primrose Hill Road and at the proposed site access road intersections were determined to be in excess of 500 feet to the north and south of each location. These values are greater than the required safe stopping sight distance (SSD) of 155 feet based on the posted speed of 25 mph, and the 200 feet for the AASHTO requirements for design speed of 30 mph established per RIDOT policy, and the 230 feet required for the actual travel speeds of 33 mph along the property frontage.

Also, as part of the safety analysis, a review of crash statistics within the study area was completed. Data was obtained from the Town of Barrington Police Department for an industry standard three-year period (2018, 2019, and 2022) to determine if any location in the immediate vicinity of the development experienced a high frequency or pattern of crashes. Due to the COVID-19 pandemic, the 2020 and 2021 data were not requested as traffic patterns and volumes were notably impacted and not representative of average traffic conditions. A total of 17 crashes occurred in the project area over the three-year study period, with no fatalities.

Summarizing the data, a majority of crashes (70%) within the study area occurred at two locations including the Middle Highway intersections with County Road and with Primrose Hill Road. At the intersection with County Road where 10 crashes occurred, the most common collision types seen were angle and rear-end collisions, which are to be expected at signal-controlled junctions due to the numerous starting and stopping movements required for the change intervals, and non-protected left turn movements. An average of three crashes per year indicates a relatively low crash frequency for an intersection requiring signalization.

Only two crashes occurred at the All -Way stop-controlled Primrose Hill Road intersection. They were both minor property damage crashes caused by driver error in traversing the junction. The remaining crashes were non-intersection along the segments of road relating to backing vehicles, an impaired driver or distracted driver.

Based upon the historical accident data obtained from the local police, and a review of existing roadway operations, geometry, physical features, and the proposed development plan, it does not appear that any significant physical safety deficiencies presently exist along the servicing roadways or at the major junctions requiring mitigation.

# 5.0 IMPACT ANALYSIS

# 5.1 TRIP GENERATION

To determine the traffic impact of a proposed development, estimates of anticipated traffic to be generated by a particular land use must be calculated. As previously discussed, the proposed residential project consists of constructing multiple residential buildings of differing sizes and types from single family homes to apartments in a village style environment, resulting in a total of 344 residential units. Additionally, the west wing of the stone manner containing approximately 20,000 square feet of available building area, will be repurposed predominately for common space accommodating neighborhood activities and management services for the village, with a limited 1,500 square foot commercial element that would be focused on a small neighborhood business use. Site amenities will also include open space and walking trails for residents. Access and egress to the site will be provided by modifying the existing driveway at its intersection with Primrose Hill Road and constructing two secondary intersections on Middle Highway for efficient access to the neighborhood. Figure 4 on the following page depicts the proposed redevelopment plan.

For this study, estimated traffic volumes for the residential redevelopment project were based on the use of trip generation data obtained from the Trip Generation Manual, an informational report published by the Institute of Transportation Engineers (ITE), a national professional organization for traffic and transportation engineers. The data provided in the ITE report are based on extensive traffic studies for numerous types of land uses (residential, commercial, industrial, etc.). This data has been found to be very reliable and provides a sound basis for estimating future trips to new development projects.

For the proposed 344 residential units, Land Use Code 210 Single Family Detached Housing (58 units), Land Use Code 215 Single Family Attached Housing (66 units), Land Use Code 220 Multifamily Housing (Low-Rise), (220 units) were reviewed for applicability in developing an estimate of site related vehicles trips. For the retail component of the project Land Use Code 822 Strip Retail Plaza (1,500 square feet) was determined to be the most applicable. Table 2 provides a summary of the peak hour volumes estimated for the proposed village type project utilizing the ITE factors. The appropriate worksheets from the manual are included in the Appendix, along with the trip estimate calculations.

## 5.2 Future Traffic Conditions

In order to properly assess the traffic impacts from a development, future traffic conditions of area roadways should be estimated for the period when the development is constructed and fully occupied. Typically, the expansion of base traffic is calculated when a project is to be constructed over an extended period, typically between three to five years. For purposes of the redevelopment project, traffic was projected over a five-year horizon.

The Town of Barrington has seen a negligible population growth over the past two decades and has declined for 16 of the last 20 years. This trend is projected to continue through 2040 by the Office of Statewide Planning as there is little available land for further development. To be conservative in our analysis, a half percent per year growth rate was utilized for the study. The half percent rate was applied to the existing volumes and then the site-specific volumes estimated for the peak hours were then added to establish the Future 2029 Build traffic condition.

In developing the intersection volumes to be analyzed under build conditions, Crossman reviewed the proposed site layout to define potential vehicle circulation patterns within the village and to the points of access/egress where a directional distribution of the site traffic was estimated. The distribution was based on journey to work data for the community and from the current traffic patterns defined from the traffic

counting program completed for this project. Based upon the location of the site and local infrastructure to major routes, it is assumed that 55/50 percent of the site trips will be oriented to/from the south and 15/25 percent of the site trips will be oriented to/from the north along Middle Highway and 30/25 percent of the site trips will be oriented to/from the east for the morning and afternoon periods respectively. The volumes were then distributed throughout the network based on existing traffic patterns at the major junctions. Figure 5 on the following page depicts the estimated future traffic volumes at the study intersections.

## 5.3 OPERATIONAL ANALYSIS

The key to any traffic impact analysis is the evaluation of roadway operations during peak traffic periods on the servicing roadway system. This condition would occur when the site-generated traffic, combined with the traffic volumes on the main roadway, result in the highest one-hour volume serviced along a roadway segment, or through an intersection. Review of record traffic data found that the weekday morning and afternoon peak hours would represent this worst-case combination of site-generated traffic with the servicing roadway peak traffic period.

The Highway Capacity Manual methodology provides the most accurate means of evaluating traffic capacity and delays for roadways and intersections. The results of this procedure are expressed in terms of Level of Service (LOS). Level of Service is a qualitative measure of traffic flow efficiency based on anticipated vehicle delays. For example, LOS "A" represents the best condition with little or no delay, while LOS "F" indicates that the roadway/intersection is at full capacity resulting in extended vehicle delays and potential queuing. Table 2 outlines the Level of Service delay criteria presented in the Highway Capacity Manual for signalized and unsignalized intersections.

TABLE 2 – Highway Capacity Criteria

| Level of Service | Unsignalized Delay<br>Per Vehicle (sec) | Signalized Delay<br>Per Vehicle (sec) |
|------------------|-----------------------------------------|---------------------------------------|
| А                | <10                                     | <10                                   |
| В                | >10 and <15                             | >10 and <20                           |
| С                | >15 and <25                             | >20 and <35                           |
| D                | >25 and <35                             | >35 and <55                           |
| Е                | >35 and <50                             | >55 and <80                           |
| F                | >50                                     | >80                                   |

**CROSSMAN ENGINEERING** 100 Jefferson Blvd., Suite 200 | Warwick, Rhode Island 02888 The Middle Highway intersections with County Road (Route 103) and Primrose Hill Road were all analyzed for the weekday morning and afternoon peak hours. The capacity analysis worksheets are included in the Appendix and Tables 3 and 4 summarize the results of the analyses for existing and future build conditions.

Table 3 on the following page depicts the current operating conditions at the study intersections. As can be seen in the table, the signalized intersection of Middle Highway with County Road currently operates overall at a good LOS B or better during the daily peak hours analyzed. No critical movements operate at less than LOS D for the signalized intersection which is considered an acceptable design condition for peak traffic conditions. At the unsignalized intersections of Middle Highway with Primrose Hill Road, all critical movements currently operate at LOS A or better with typically one to two vehicles queued on the approaches during the peak daily traffic periods.

<sup>(</sup>S) – Signalized

Table 4 presents the future design period taking into consideration base traffic growth along the servicing roadways while also adding in the new trips generated by the proposed residential development. The results of the signalized analysis determined that the Middle Highway intersection with County Road will continue to operate overall at a good LOS B during the daily peak hours of traffic similar to existing operations with only minor increases in delays of a few seconds for many of the movements.

![](_page_20_Picture_9.jpeg)

<sup>(</sup>U) – Unsignalized

The unsignalized intersection of Middle Highway with Primrose Hill Road is proposed to be upgraded in the build scenario to include a modified eastbound approach from the site, converting the intersection to a more conventional four-legged intersection as the former driveway will be become a local road servicing the neighborhood. The new accessroad approach is proposed to be stop-controlled and assumed to have one lane for all movements. As can be seen in the table, under future build conditions, the junction will continue to operate with all movements operating at a good LOS A with delays of under 10 seconds and typically only one to two vehicles queued on an approach waiting to traverse the intersection. A review of the proposed site access road intersections also found that they are estimated to operate at a good LOS A with acceptable delays of minor approach access road traffic, and no congestion.

# 6.0 CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

In summary, the study has shown that the proposed residential redevelopment project access and circulation plan has been designed to provide a level of traffic safety and efficiency on the servicing roadway system. The safety of the study intersections of Middle Highway with Primrose Hill Road and the Site Access Roads were reviewed for geometry and sight distances. The study intersections were determined to provide sufficient sight distances in accordance with AASHTO criteria for visibility and decision making of drivers attempting to enter/exit main street traffic from the development. Analysis of the crash data along the servicing roadway determined there was no frequency or severity of crashes within the study area requiring safety enhancements.

One element that should be investigate further with development of the subject property is the accommodations for pedestrian circulation within and to the new village neighborhood. Based upon review of the preliminary site plans, sidewalks and walking trails are proposed within the new neighborhood providing for safe and adequate pedestrian access through the development. The connection to the local street sidewalk infrastructure though, requires further coordination with town and state officials as there are missing segments along Middle Highway as previously noted. New sidewalks and crosswalks can be installed strategically along the adjacent roads including improvements proposed at the Primrose Hill Road intersection required at the existing driveway access. These improvements can provide linkage and connectivity of pedestrian facilities in the immediate vicinity of the development to maintain a safe pedestrian environment within and adjacent to the Belton Court Villag<sup>e</sup> project.

The results of the operational analysis determined that the estimated minor increase in traffic during the daily peak periods resulting from the proposed residential project will have a minor impact on overall traffic operations along the servicing roadways and intersections, particularly during the daily morning and afternoon peak hours when the residential neighborhood would generate the highest daily traffic volumes. Existing intersections will maintain a good level of service with a minor increase in delays and acceptable operations. The proposed site access road intersections are estimated to operate in a safe and efficient manner.

Therefore, based upon the data collected on the servicing roadways, the analysis completed as part of this study including recommendations, the proposed residential redevelopment project was determined to have adequate and safe access to a public street, and will not have an adverse impact on public safety and welfare in the study area.

ITE Land Use Code 210 - Single-Family Detached Housing

# Land Use: 210 Single-Family Detached Housing

### **Description**

A single-family detached housing site includes any single-family detached home on an individual lot. A typical site surveyed is a suburban subdivision.

## **Specialized Land Use**

Data have been submitted for several single-family detached housing developments with homes that are commonly referred to as patio homes. A patio home is a detached housing unit that is located on a small lot with little (or no) front or back yard. In some subdivisions, communal maintenance of outside grounds is provided for the patio homes. The three patio home sites total 299 dwelling units with overall weighted average trip generation rates of 5.35 vehicle trips per dwelling unit for weekday, 0.26 for the AM adjacent street peak hour, and 0.47 for the PM adjacent street peak hour. These patio home rates based on a small sample of sites are lower than those for single-family detached housing (Land Use 210), lower than those for single-family attached housing (Land Use 251), and higher than those for senior adult housing -- single-family (Land Use 251). Further analysis of this housing type will be conducted in a future edition of Trip Generation Manual.

### **Additional Data**

The technical appendices provide supporting information on time-of-day distributions for this land use. The appendices can be accessed through either the ITETripGen web app or the trip generation resource page on the ITE website (https://www.ite.org/technical-resources/topics/tripand-parking-generation/).

For 30 of the study sites, data on the number of residents and number of household vehicles are available. The overall averages for the 30 sites are 3.6 residents per dwelling unit and 1.5 vehicles per dwelling unit.

The sites were surveyed in the 1980s, the 1990s, the 2000s, and the 2010s in Arizona, California, Connecticut, Delaware, Illinois, Indiana, Kentucky, Maryland, Massachusetts, Minnesota, Montana, New Jersey, North Carolina, Ohio, Ontario (CAN), Oregon, Pennsylvania, South Carolina, South Dakota, Tennessee, Vermont, Virginia, and West Virginia.

### **Source Numbers**

100, 105, 114, 126, 157, 167, 177, 197, 207, 211, 217, 267, 275, 293, 300, 319, 320, 356, 357, 367, 384, 387, 407, 435, 522, 550, 552, 579, 598, 601, 603, 614, 637, 711, 716, 720, 728, 735, 868, 869, 903, 925, 936, 1005, 1007, 1008, 1010, 1033, 1066, 1077, 1078, 1079

# Land Use: 215 **Single-Family Attached Housing**

### **Description**

Single-family attached housing includes any single-family housing unit that shares a wall with an adjoining dwelling unit, whether the walls are for living space, a vehicle garage, or storage space.

### **Additional Data**

The database for this land use includes duplexes (defined as a single structure with two distinct dwelling units, typically joined side-by-side and each with at least one outside entrance) and townhouses/rowhouses (defined as a single structure with three or more distinct dwelling units, joined side-by-side in a row and each with an outside entrance).

The technical appendices provide supporting information on time-of-day distributions for this land use. The appendices can be accessed through either the ITETripGen web app or the trip generation resource page on the ITE website (https://www.ite.org/technical-resources/topics/tripand-parking-generation/).

The sites were surveyed in the 1980s, the 1990s, the 2000s, and the 2010s in British Columbia (CAN), California, Georgia, Illinois, Maryland, Massachusetts, Minnesota, New Jersey, Ontario (CAN), Oregon, Pennsylvania, South Dakota, Utah, Virginia, and Wisconsin.

### **Source Numbers**

168, 204, 211, 237, 305, 306, 319, 321, 357, 390, 418, 525, 571, 583, 638, 735, 868, 869, 870, 896, 912, 959, 1009, 1046, 1056, 1058, 1077

ITE Land Use Code 220 – Multifamily (Low-Rise)

# Land Use: 220 Multifamily Housing (Low-Rise)

### **Description**

Low-rise multifamily housing includes apartments, townhouses, and condominiums located within the same building with at least three other dwelling units and that have two or three floors (levels). Various configurations fit this description, including walkup apartment, mansion apartment, and stacked townhouse.

- A walkup apartment typically is two or three floors in height with dwelling units that are accessed by a single or multiple entrances with stairways and hallways.
- A mansion apartment is a single structure that contains several apartments within what appears to be a single-family dwelling unit.
- A fourplex is a single two-story structure with two matching dwelling units on the ground and second floors. Access to the individual units is typically internal to the structure and provided through a central entry and stairway.
- A stacked townhouse is designed to match the external appearance of a townhouse. But, unlike a townhouse dwelling unit that only shares walls with an adjoining unit, the stacked townhouse units share both floors and walls. Access to the individual units is typically internal to the structure and provided through a central entry and stairway.

Multifamily housing (mid-rise) (Land Use 221), multifamily housing (high-rise) (Land Use 222), affordable housing (Land Use 223), and off-campus student apartment (low-rise) (Land Use 225) are related land uses.

### Land Use Subcategory

Data are presented for two subcategories for this land use: (1) not close to rail transit and (2) close to rail transit. A site is considered close to rail transit if the walking distance between the residential site entrance and the closest rail transit station entrance is ½ mile or less.

### **Additional Data**

For the three sites for which both the number of residents and the number of occupied dwelling units were available, there were an average of 2.72 residents per occupied dwelling unit.

For the two sites for which the numbers of both total dwelling units and occupied dwelling units were available, an average of 96.2 percent of the total dwelling units were occupied.

The technical appendices provide supporting information on time-of-day distributions for this land use. The appendices can be accessed through either the ITETripGen web app or the trip

![](_page_101_Picture_14.jpeg)

generation resource page on the ITE website (https://www.ite.org/technical-resources/topics/tripand-parking-generation/).

For the three sites for which data were provided for both occupied dwelling units and residents, there was an average of 2.72 residents per occupied dwelling unit.

It is expected that the number of bedrooms and number of residents are likely correlated to the trips generated by a residential site. To assist in future analysis, trip generation studies of all multifamily housing should attempt to obtain information on occupancy rate and on the mix of residential unit sizes (i.e., number of units by number of bedrooms at the site complex).

The sites were surveyed in the 1980s, the 1990s, the 2000s, the 2010s, and the 2020s in British Columbia (CAN), California, Delaware, Florida, Georgia, Illinois, Indiana, Maine, Maryland, Massachusetts, Minnesota, New Jersey, Ontario (CAN), Oregon, Pennsylvania, South Carolina, South Dakota, Tennessee, Texas, Utah, and Washington.

### **Source Numbers**

188, 204, 237, 300, 305, 306, 320, 321, 357, 390, 412, 525, 530, 579, 583, 638, 864, 866, 896, 901, 903, 904, 936, 939, 944, 946, 947, 948, 963, 964, 966, 967, 1012, 1013, 1014, 1036, 1047, 1056, 1071, 1076

ITE Land Use Code 822 – Strip Retail Plaza

# Land Use: 822 Strip Retail Plaza (<40k)

# **Description**

A strip retail plaza is an integrated group of commercial establishments that is planned, developed, owned, and managed as a unit. Each study site in this land use has less than 40,000 square feet of gross leasable area (GLA). Because a strip retail plaza is open-air, the GLA is the same as the gross floor area of the building.

The 40,000 square feet GFA threshold between strip retail plaza and shopping plaza (Land Use 821) was selected based on an examination of the overall shopping center/plaza database. No shopping plaza with a supermarket as its anchor is smaller than 40,000 square feet GLA.

Shopping center (>150k) (Land use 820), shopping plaza (40-150k) (Land Use 821), and factory outlet center (Land Use 823) are related uses.

# **Additional Data**

The technical appendices provide supporting information on time-of-day distributions for this land use. The appendices can be accessed through either the ITETripGen web app or the trip generation resource page on the ITE website (https://www.ite.org/technical-resources/topics/tripand-parking-generation/).

The sites were surveyed in the 1980s, the 1990s, the 2000s, and the 2010s in Alberta (CAN), California, Delaware, Florida, New Jersey, Ontario (CAN), South Dakota, Vermont, Washington, and Wisconsin.

# **Source Numbers**

304, 358, 423, 428, 437, 507, 715, 728, 936, 960, 961, 974, 1009
